# Searching Sourcegraph

Search before you build. Existing patterns reduce tokens, ensure consistency, and surface tested solutions.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Tool Selection Logic

**Start here:**

1. **Know the exact symbol or pattern?** → `sg_keyword_search`
2. **Know the concept, not the code?** → `sg_nls_search`
3. **Need to understand how/why?** → `sg_deepsearch_read`
4. **Tracing a symbol's usage?** → `sg_find_references`
5. **Need full implementation?** → `sg_go_to_definition` → `sg_read_file`

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Exact code patterns | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Trace usage | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Understand systems | `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Read files | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
file:.*\.test\.ts$ -file:__mocks__   # Tests, exclude mocks
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

Combine filters: `repo:^github.com/myorg/backend$ file:src/handlers lang:typescript`

## Context-Aware Behaviour

**When the user provides a file path or error message:**
- Extract symbols, function names, or error codes
- Search for those exact terms first
- Trace references if the error involves a known symbol

**When the user asks "how does X work":**
- Prefer `sg_deepsearch_read` for architectural understanding
- Follow up with `sg_read_file` on key files mentioned in the response

**When the user is implementing a new feature:**
- Search for similar existing implementations first
- Read tests for usage examples
- Check for shared utilities before creating new ones

**When debugging:**
- Search for the exact error string
- Trace the symbol where the error is thrown
- Check recent changes with `sg_diff_search`

## Workflows

For detailed step-by-step workflows, see:
- `workflows/implementing-feature.md` — when building new features
- `workflows/understanding-code.md` — when exploring unfamiliar systems
- `workflows/debugging-issue.md` — when tracking down bugs

## Efficiency Rules

**Minimise tool calls:**
- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms (faster, more precise)

**Batch your understanding:**
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than reading one and asking questions
- Use `sg_deepsearch_read` for "how does X work" instead of multiple keyword searches

**Avoid common token waste:**
- Don't search all repos when you know the target repo
- Don't use `sg_deepsearch_read` for simple "find all" queries
- Don't re-read files you've already seen in this conversation

## Query Patterns

| Intent | Query |
|--------|-------|
| React hooks | `file:.*\.tsx$ use[A-Z].*= \(` |
| API routes | `file:src/api app\.(get\|post\|put\|delete)` |
| Error handling | `catch.*Error\|\.catch\(` |
| Type definitions | `file:types/ export (interface\|type)` |
| Test setup | `file:.*\.test\. beforeEach\|beforeAll` |
| Config files | `file:(webpack\|vite\|rollup)\.config` |
| CI/CD | `file:\.github/workflows deploy` |

For more patterns, see `query-patterns.md`.

## Output Formatting

**Search results:**
- Present as a brief summary, not raw tool output
- Highlight the most relevant file and line
- Include a code snippet only if it directly answers the question

**Code explanations:**
- Start with a one-sentence summary
- Use the codebase's own terminology
- Reference specific files and functions

**Recommendations:**
- Present as numbered steps if actionable
- Link to specific patterns found in the codebase
- Note any existing utilities that should be reused

## Common Mistakes

| Mistake | Fix |
|---------|-----|
| Searching all repos | Add `repo:^github.com/org/repo$` |
| Too many results | Add `file:` pattern or keywords |
| Missing relevant code | Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching |
| Not understanding context | Use `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Guessing patterns | Read implementations with `sg_read_file` |

## Principles

- Start narrow, expand if needed
- Chain tools: search → read → find references → definition
- Check tests for usage examples
- Read before generating

# Task: Kubernetes client-go Systems Guide

**Repository:** kubernetes/kubernetes (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce an advanced systems guide for client-go that explains API access, controller loops, and extension paths.

## Scope

Focus on the client-go subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

     1→# `client-go` Architecture
     2→
     3→This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the
     4→major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.
     5→
     6→## Client Configuration
     7→
     8→There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The
     9→`rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The
    10→`tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the
    11→complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external
    12→authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).
    13→
    14→## REST Client
    15→
    16→The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates
    17→the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,
    18→Kubernetes-specific object logic.
    19→
    20→The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a
    21→chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific
    22→task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication
    23→is injected into requests.
    24→
    25→The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring
    26→response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to
    27→supporting different client models from a common base.
    28→
    29→### Endpoint Interactions
    30→
    31→*   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format
    32→    (JSON or Protobuf). A 
... (truncated)

The documentation has been written to `/workspace/documentation.md`. Here is a summary of what it covers:

**Structure**: Seven sections with concrete file citations throughout.

**Section 1 — Subsystem Purpose and Dependencies**: Places client-go in the Kubernetes dependency graph, identifies its upstream (apimachinery, api) and downstream consumers (all in-tree controllers, kubectl, third-party operators), and explains the versioning contract (`v0.X.Y` tracks `v1.X.Y`).

**Section 2 — Key Components**: Describes each major package — `rest` (HTTP transport + request builder), `kubernetes` (typed clientset), `dynamic` (unstructured client for CRDs), `discovery`, `tools/cache` (controller infrastructure), `util/workqueue`, `tools/leaderelection`, and `tools/clientcmd` — with specific responsibility boundaries.

**Section 3 — End-to-End Control Flow**: Traces two complete paths with exact line number references:
- How a typed API call travels through the rate limiter, transport chain, and response deserializer.
- The full informer pipeline (Reflector → DeltaFIFO → Indexer → sharedProcessor → work queue → reconcile loop), including the WatchList optimization, bookmark handling, missed deletion recovery, and the late-joining handler protocol.

**Section 4 — Failure Modes and Tradeoffs**: Covers watch expiry and the relist cycle, missed deletions during relist, slow handler backpressure, client-side rate limiting traps, leader election's lack of hard exclusivity, and Indexer mutation safety.

**Section 5 — Extension Points**: Documents `WrapTransport`, `TransformFunc`, custom indexers, `WatchErrorHandler`, `ListerWatcher` substitution, custom work queue rate limiters, and Server-Side Apply — each with the specific risks associated with misuse.

5. Read @ -
Input
{
  "file_path": "/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md"
}
Output
{
  "file": {
    "content": "# `client-go` Architecture\n\nThis document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\nmajor components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n\n## Client Configuration\n\nThere is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n`rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n`tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\ncomplex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\nauthentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n\n## REST Client\n\nThe `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\nthe low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\nKubernetes-specific object logic.\n\nThe `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\nchain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\ntask, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\nis injected into requests.\n\nThe client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\nresponse processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\nsupporting different client models from a common base.\n\n### Endpoint Interactions\n\n*   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n    (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n    request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n    Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n*   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n    object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n    `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n*   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n    return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n    responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n    Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n*   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n    `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n    objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n    the underlying streaming protocol.\n\n### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n\n*   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n    `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n*   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n    `WarningHandler`.\n*   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n    client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n*   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n    responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n\n## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n\nTo handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\nmodels.\n\nThe **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n\nThe **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\nto interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n    **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n    resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n\n## Code Generation\n\nA core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\nstrongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\ncontroller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\nseveral key components:\n\n*   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n*   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n*   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n*   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n\nA contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\nof these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n\n`sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n\n## Controller Infrastructure\n\nThe `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\nrequest-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n\nThe data flow is as follows:\n\n```mermaid\ngraph TD\n    subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n        API_Server[API Server]\n    end\n\n    subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n        Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n        DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n        Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n        EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n    end\n\n    subgraph \"User Code\"\n        WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n        Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n    end\n\n    API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n    Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n    DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n    Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n    EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n    WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n    Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n```\n\nA **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n`resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\nstream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n**\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n`Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n`resourceVersion` to restart from.\n\nThe **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\nlogic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n\n## Controller Patterns\n\nThe controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\nensure resiliency.\n\nThe **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\nand reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\nwork queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\nblocking the informer's watch stream.\n\nFor high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\nsolution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\nshared `Lease` object.\n\n## Server-Side Apply\n\n`client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\nserver's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\nmodel that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n**`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\nconstruct the declarative patch.\n\n## Versioning and Compatibility\n\n`client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\nversion `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n\nThe Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\nversion of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\nA contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n",
    "filePath": "/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md",
    "numLines": 163,
    "startLine": 1,
    "totalLines": 163
  },
  "type": "text"
}

An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\\n43: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\\n44: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\\n45: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\\n46: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\\n47: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\\n48: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\\n49: //\\n50: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\\n51: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\\n52: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\\n53: // other appropriate content or (2) \\\"absent\\\".\\n54: //\\n55: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\\n56: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\\n57: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\\n58: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\\n59: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\\n60: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\\n61: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\\n62: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\\n63: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\\n64: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\\n65: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\\n66: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\\n67: // selector.\\n68: //\\n69: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\\n70: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\\n71: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\\n72: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\\n73: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\\n74: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\\n75: //\\n76: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\\n77: // during `Run()`.\\n78: //\\n79: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\\n80: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\\n81: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\\n82: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\\n83: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\\n84: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\\n85: // persistently thwart achievement).\\n86: //\\n87: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\\n88: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\\n89: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\\n90: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\\n91: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\\n92: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\\n93: //\\n94: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\\n95: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\\n96: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\\n97: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\\n98: // into its constituent parts.\\n99: //\\n100: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\\n101: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\\n102: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\\n103: //\\n104: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\\n105: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\\n106: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\\n107: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\\n108: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\\n109: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\\n110: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\\n111: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\\n112: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\\n113: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\\n114: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\\n115: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\\n116: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\\n117: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\\n118: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\\n119: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\\n120: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\\n121: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\\n122: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\\n123: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\\n124: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\\n125: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\\n126: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\\n127: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\\n128: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\\n129: //\\n130: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\\n131: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\\n132: // notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\\n133: // to something else, for example through a\\n134: // `client-go/util/workqueue`.\\n135: //\\n136: // A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\\n137: // state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\\n138: // ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\\n139: type SharedInformer interface {\\n140: \\t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\\n141: \\t// the shared informer's resync period.  Events to a single handler are\\n142: \\t// delivered sequentially, but there is no coordination between\\n143: \\t// different handlers.\\n144: \\t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to\\n145: \\t// remove the handler again, or to tell if the handler is synced (has\\n146: \\t// seen every item in the initial list).\\n147: \\t//\\n148: \\t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandler in code which supports contextual logging.\\n149: \\tAddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\\n150: \\t// AddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod adds an event handler to the\\n151: \\t// shared informer with the requested resync period; zero means\\n152: \\t// this handler does not care about resyncs.  The resync operation\\n153: \\t// consists of delivering to the handler an update notification\\n154: \\t// for every object in the informer's local cache; it does not add\\n155: \\t// any interactions with the authoritative storage.  Some\\n156: \\t// informers do no resyncs at all, not even for handlers added\\n157: \\t// with a non-zero resyncPeriod.  For an informer that does\\n158: \\t// resyncs, and for each handler that requests resyncs, that\\n159: \\t// informer develops a nominal resync period that is no shorter\\n160: \\t// than the requested period but may be longer.  The actual time\\n161: \\t// between any two resyncs may be longer than the nominal period\\n162: \\t// because the implementation takes time to do work and there may\\n163: \\t// be competing load and scheduling noise.\\n164: \\t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to remove\\n165: \\t// the handler again and an error if the handler cannot be added.\\n166:     "content": "1: /*\n2: Copyright 2015 The Kubernetes Authors.\n3: \n4: Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \"License\");\n5: you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\n6: You may obtain a copy of the License at\n7: \n8:     http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n9: \n10: Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n11: distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n12: WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n13: See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n14: limitations under the License.\n15: */\n16: \n17: package cache\n18: \n19: import (\n20: \t\"context\"\n21: \t\"errors\"\n22: \t\"fmt\"\n23: \t\"sync\"\n24: \t\"time\"\n25: \n26: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/api/meta\"\n27: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/runtime\"\n28: \tutilruntime \"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/runtime\"\n29: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/wait\"\n30: \t\"k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/synctrack\"\n31: \t\"k8s.io/utils/buffer\"\n32: \t\"k8s.io/utils/clock\"\n33: \t\"k8s.io/utils/ptr\"\n34: \n35: \t\"k8s.io/klog/v2\"\n36: \n37: \tclientgofeaturegate \"k8s.io/client-go/features\"\n38: )\n39: \n40: // SharedInformer provides eventually consistent linkage of its\n41: // clients to the authoritative state of a given collection of\n42: // objects.  An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\n43: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\n44: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\n45: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\n46: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\n47: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\n48: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\n49: //\n50: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\n51: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\n52: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\n53: // other appropriate content or (2) \"absent\".\n54: //\n55: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\n56: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\n57: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\n58: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\n59: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\n60: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\n61: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\n62: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\n63: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\n64: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\n65: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\n66: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\n67: // selector.\n68: //\n69: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\n70: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\n71: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\n72: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\n73: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\n74: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\n75: //\n76: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\n77: // during `Run()`.\n78: //\n79: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\n80: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\n81: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\n82: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\n83: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\n84: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\n85: // persistently thwart achievement).\n86: //\n87: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\n88: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\n89: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\n90: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\n91: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\n92: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\n93: //\n94: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\n95: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\n96: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\n97: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\n98: // into its constituent parts.\n99: //\n100: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\n101: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\n102: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\n103: //\n104: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\n105: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\n106: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\n107: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\n108: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\n109: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\n110: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\n111: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\n112: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\n113: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\n114: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\n115: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\n116: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\n117: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\n118: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\n119: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\n120: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\n121: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\n122: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\n123: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\n124: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\n125: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\n126: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\n127: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\n128: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\n129: //\n130: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\n131: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\n132: // notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\n133: // to something else, for example through a\n134: // `client-go/util/workqueue`.\n135: //\n136: // A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\n137: // state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\n138: // ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\n139: type SharedInformer interface {\n140: \t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\n141: \t// the shared informer's resync period.  Events to a single handler are\n142: \t// delivered sequentially, but there is no coordination between\n143: \t// different handlers.\n144: \t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to\n145: \t// remove the handler again, or to tell if the handler is synced (has\n146: \t// seen every item in the initial list).\n147: \t//\n148: \t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandler in code which supports contextual logging.\n149: \tAddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\n150: \t// AddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod adds an event handler to the\n151: \t// shared informer with the requested resync period; zero means\n152: \t// this handler does not care about resyncs.  The resync operation\n153: \t// consists of delivering to the handler an update notification\n154: \t// for every object in the informer's local cache; it does not add\n155: \t// any interactions with the authoritative storage.  Some\n156: \t// informers do no resyncs at all, not even for handlers added\n157: \t// with a non-zero resyncPeriod.  For an informer that does\n158: \t// resyncs, and for each handler that requests resyncs, that\n159: \t// informer develops a nominal resync period that is no shorter\n160: \t// than the requested period but may be longer.  The actual time\n161: \t// between any two resyncs may be longer than the nominal period\n162: \t// because the implementation takes time to do work and there may\n163: \t// be competing load and scheduling noise.\n164: \t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to remove\n165: \t// the handler again and an error if the handler cannot be added.\n166: \t//\n167: \t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod in code which supports contextual logging.\n168: \tAddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod(handler ResourceEventHandler, resyncPeriod time.Duration) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\n169: \t// AddEventHandlerWithOptions is a variant of When the queue is shutdown new\n225: // items will silently be ignored and not queued or marked as dirty for\n226: // reprocessing.\n227: func (q *Typed[T]) Add(item T) {\n228: \tq.cond.L.Lock()\n229: \tdefer q.cond.L.Unlock()\n230: \tif q.shuttingDown {\n231: \t\treturn\n232: \t}\n233: \tif q.dirty.Has(item) {\n234: \t\t// the same item is added again before it is processed, call the Touch\n235: \t\t// function if the queue cares about it (for e.g, reset its priority)\n236: \t\tif !q.processing.Has(item) {\n237: \t\t\tq.queue.Touch(item)\n238: \t\t}\n239: \t\treturn\n240: \t}\n241: \n242: \tq.metrics.add(item)\n243: \n244: \tq.dirty.Insert(item)\n245: \tif q.processing.Has(item) {\n246: \t\treturn\n247: \t}\n248: \n249: \tq.queue.Push(item)\n250: \tq.cond.Signal()\n251: }\n252: \n253: // Len returns the current queue length, for informational purposes only. You\n254: // shouldn't e.g. gate a call to Add() or Get() on Len() being a particular\n255: // value, that can't be synchronized properly.\n256: func (q *Typed[T]) Len() int {\n257: \tq.cond.L.Lock()\n258: \tdefer q.cond.L.Unlock()\n259: \treturn q.queue.Len()\n260: }\n261: \n262: // Get blocks until it can return an item to be processed. If shutdown = true,\n263: // the caller should end their goroutine. You must call Done with item when you\n264: // have finished processing it.\n265: func (q *Typed[T]) Get() (item T, shutdown bool) {\n266: \tq.cond.L.Lock()\n267: \tdefer q.cond.L.Unlock()\n268: \tfor q.queue.Len() == 0 && !q.shuttingDown {\n269: \t\tq.cond.Wait()\n270: \t}\n271: \tif q.queue.Len() == 0 {\n272: \t\t// We must be shutting down.\n273: \t\treturn *new(T), true\n274: \t}\n275: \n276: \titem = q.queue.Pop()\n277: \n278: \tq.metrics.get(item)\n279: \n280: \tq.processing.Insert(item)\n281: \tq.dirty.Delete(item)\n282: \n283: \treturn item, false\n284: }\n285: \n286: // Done marks item as done processing, and if it has been marked as dirty again\n287: // while it was being processed, it will be re-added to the queue for\n288: // re-processing.\n289: func (q *Typed[T]) Done(item T) {\n290: \tq.cond.L.Lock()\n291: \tdefer q.cond.L.Unlock()\n292: \n293: \tq.metrics.done(item)\n294: \n295: \tq.processing.Delete(item)\n296: \tif q.dirty.Has(item) {\n297: \t\tq.queue.Push(item)\n298: \t\tq.cond.Signal()\n299: \t} else if q.processing.Len() == 0 {\n300: \t\tq.cond.Signal()\n301: \t}\n302: }\n303: \n304: // ShutDown will cause q to ignore all new items added to it. Worker\n305: // goroutines will continue processing items in the queue until it is\n306: // empty and then receive the shutdown signal.\n307: func (q *Typed[T]) ShutDown() {\n308: \tdefer q.wg.Wait()\n309: \tq.stopOnce.Do(func() {\n310: \t\tdefer close(q.stopCh)\n311: \t})\n312: \n313: \tq.cond.L.Lock()\n314: \tdefer q.cond.L.Unlock()\n315: \n316: \tq.drain = false\n317: \tq.shuttingDown = true\n318: \tq.cond.Broadcast()\n319: }\n320: \n321: // ShutDownWithDrain is equivalent to ShutDown but waits until all items\n322: // in the queue have been processed.\n323: // ShutDown can be called after ShutDownWithDrain to force\n324: // ShutDownWithDrain to stop waiting.\n325: // Workers must call Done on an item after processing it, otherwise\n326: // ShutDownWithDrain will block indefinitely.\n327: func (q *Typed[T]) ShutDownWithDrain() {\n328: \tdefer q.wg.Wait()\n329: \tq.stopOnce.Do(func() {\n330: \t\tdefer close(q.stopCh)\n331: \t})\n332: \tq.cond.L.Lock()\n333: \tdefer q.cond.L.Unlock()\n334: \n335: \tq.drain = true\n336: \tq.shuttingDown = true\n337: \tq.cond.Broadcast()\n338: \n339: \tfor q.processing.Len() != 0 && q.drain {\n340: \t\tq.cond.Wait()\n341: \t}\n342: }\n343: \n344: func (q *Typed[T]) ShuttingDown() bool {\n345: \tq.cond.L.Lock()\n346: \tdefer q.cond.L.Unlock()\n347: \n348: \treturn q.shuttingDown\n349: }\n350: \n351: func (q *Typed[T]) updateUnfinishedWork() {\n352: \tq.cond.L.Lock()\n353: \tdefer q.cond.L.Unlock()\n354: \tif !q.shuttingDown {\n355: \t\tq.metrics.updateUnfinishedWork()\n356: \t}\n357: }\n358: \n359: func (q *Typed[T]) updateUnfinishedWorkLoop() {\n360: \tt := q.clock.NewTicker(q.unfinishedWorkUpdatePeriod)\n361: \tdefer t.Stop()\n362: \tfor {\n363: \t\tselect {\n364: \t\tcase <-t.C():\n365: \t\t\tq.updateUnfinishedWork()\n366: \t\tcase <-q.stopCh:\n367: \t\t\treturn\n368: \t\t}\n369: \t}\n370: }\n371: ",

This is measured against time of\\n122: \\t// last observed ack.\\n123: \\t//\\n124: \\t// A client needs to wait a full LeaseDuration without observing a change to\\n125: \\t// the record before it can attempt to take over. When all clients are\\n126: \\t// shutdown and a new set of clients are started with different names against\\n127: \\t// the same leader record, they must wait the full LeaseDuration before\\n128: \\t// attempting to acquire the lease. Thus LeaseDuration should be as short as\\n129: \\t// possible (within your tolerance for clock skew rate) to avoid a possible\\n130: \\t// long waits in the scenario.\\n131: \\t//\\n132: \\t// Core clients default this value to 15 seconds.\\n133: \\tLeaseDuration time.Duration\\n134: \\t// RenewDeadline is the duration that the acting master will retry\\n135: \\t// refreshing leadership before giving up.\\n136: \\t//\\n137: \\t// Core clients default this value to 10 seconds.\\n138: \\tRenewDeadline time.Duration\\n139: \\t// RetryPeriod is the duration the LeaderElector clients should wait\\n140: \\t// between tries of actions.\\n141: \\t//\\n142: \\t// Core clients default this value to 2 seconds.\\n143: \\tRetryPeriod time.Duration\\n144: \\n145: \\t// Callbacks are callbacks that are triggered during certain lifecycle\\n146: \\t// events of the LeaderElector\\n147: \\tCallbacks LeaderCallbacks\\n148: \\n149: \\t// WatchDog is the associated health checker\\n150: \\t// WatchDog may be null if it's not needed/configured.\\n151: \\tWatchDog *HealthzAdaptor\\n152: \\n153: \\t// ReleaseOnCancel should be set true if the lock should be released\\n154: \\t// when the run context is cancelled.   "content": "# client-go: Advanced Systems Guide\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies\n\n`client-go` is the official Go client library for the Kubernetes API. It occupies a well-defined position in the Kubernetes dependency graph: it sits above the network and serialization layers (`k8s.io/apimachinery`, `k8s.io/api`) and below every component that reads from or writes to the cluster (controllers, the scheduler, kubeadm, kubelet, kubectl). All in-tree controllers\u2014`kube-controller-manager`, `kube-scheduler`, `kubelet`, the cloud controller\u2014use it directly.\n\nThe library lives in the Kubernetes monorepo under `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/` and is periodically published to `k8s.io/client-go`. The staging layout means that changes to core API types in `pkg/apis/` are made there first, then the code generator rebuilds the corresponding client code inside `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/`.\n\n**Upstream dependencies** (what client-go consumes):\n- `k8s.io/apimachinery` \u2014 API object model, resource versioning, watch types, serialization primitives\n- `k8s.io/api` \u2014 concrete typed API structs (Pod, Deployment, etc.)\n- Standard library `net/http`, `golang.org/x/net/http2`\n\n**Downstream consumers** (what depends on client-go):\n- Every in-tree controller (`cmd/kube-controller-manager/app/`)\n- `cmd/kube-scheduler/app/`\n- `cmd/kubelet/app/`\n- `cmd/kubectl/`\n- All third-party operators and controllers written against the Kubernetes API\n\n**Versioning contract**: `client-go` version `v0.X.Y` tracks Kubernetes `v1.X.Y`. A client built with an older version works with a newer API server (forward compatibility is guaranteed); the reverse is not.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and Responsibility Split\n\nThe library is factored into a set of packages with strict responsibility boundaries. Understanding which package owns which concern prevents common misuse.\n\n### 2.1 `rest` \u2014 HTTP Transport and Request Building\n\n**Files**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/config.go`, `rest/request.go`, `rest/client.go`, `rest/transport.go`\n\n`rest.Config` is the root configuration object. Every other client is ultimately constructed from it:\n\n```go\n// rest/config.go:55\ntype Config struct {\n    Host       string\n    BearerToken string `datapolicy:\"token\"`\n    TLSClientConfig\n    QPS     float32  // default 5.0\n    Burst   int      // default 10\n    RateLimiter flowcontrol.RateLimiter\n    WrapTransport transport.WrapperFunc\n    ...\n}\n```\n\n`RESTClientFor(config)` materialises this into a `*RESTClient`:\n1. It calls `HTTPClientFor(config)`, which assembles an `http.Client` whose `Transport` is a chain of `http.RoundTripper` wrappers built by `transport.New()` (`rest/transport.go`).\n2. It creates a `flowcontrol.NewTokenBucketRateLimiter` at the configured QPS/Burst if no custom `RateLimiter` is set (`config.go:381`).\n3. It wraps everything in `NewRESTClient`.\n\n`rest.Request` implements a fluent builder pattern:\n```go\n// rest/request.go:96\ntype Request struct {\n    c *RESTClient\n    // ... verb, resource, namespace, name, body, params ...\n}\n```\nCallers chain `.Namespace(\"default\").Resource(\"pods\").Name(\"my-pod\").Get()`. The actual HTTP call is deferred until a terminal method like `.Do(ctx)`, `.Into(&pod)`, or `.Watch(ctx)` is called.\n\nThe transport layer (`transport/transport.go`) enforces a minimum TLS version of 1.2, constructs the `tls.Config`, and wires in authentication `RoundTripper`s (bearer token injection, certificate auth, exec-based credential plugins). The `WrapTransport` field on `rest.Config` allows callers to insert additional middleware into this chain.\n\n### 2.2 `kubernetes` \u2014 Typed Clientset\n\n**Files**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/kubernetes/`\n\nThe `kubernetes.Clientset` provides compile-time-safe access to every built-in API group. It is code-generated. Each group gets a dedicated client (e.g., `CoreV1()`, `AppsV1()`, `BatchV1()`) that in turn returns resource-scoped interfaces (`PodsGetter`, `DeploymentsGetter`). These are thin wrappers around `RESTClient` calls\u2014they translate Go struct parameters into the wire format using the NegotiatedSerializer, set the correct `GroupVersion` path, and unmarshal responses.\n\n### 2.3 `dynamic` \u2014 Unstructured Client\n\n**File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/dynamic/interface.go`\n\n```go\n// dynamic/interface.go:29\ntype Interface interface {\n    Resource(resource schema.GroupVersionResource) NamespaceableResourceInterface\n}\ntype ResourceInterface interface {\n    Create(ctx context.Context, obj *unstructured.Unstructured, ...) (*unstructured.Unstructured, error)\n    Apply(ctx context.Context, name string, obj *unstructured.Unstructured, ...) (*unstructured.Unstructured, error)\n    Watch(ctx context.Context, opts metav1.ListOptions) (watch.Interface, error)\n    ...\n}\n```\n\nThe dynamic client takes a `GroupVersionResource` at call time (not compile time), making it suitable for CRDs, admission webhooks, and any consumer that needs to interact with arbitrary resource types without code generation. All objects are returned as `*unstructured.Unstructured` maps.\n\n### 2.4 `discovery` \u2014 API Server Capability Query\n\n**Directory**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/discovery/`\n\nThe `DiscoveryClient` queries `/api` and `/apis` to enumerate the resource types the server exposes. `CachedDiscoveryClient` wraps it and persists the results to disk (via `~/.kube/cache`), since discovery calls are expensive and rarely change. This is the mechanism by which `dynamic` clients know whether a given GVR exists, and it underpins `kubectl`'s command-line expansion.\n\n### 2.5 `tools/cache` \u2014 Controller Infrastructure\n\n**Directory**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/`\n\nThis package is the most consequential part of client-go for controller authors. It replaces a request-per-event pattern with an event-driven, cached architecture. Its components are described in Section 3.\n\n### 2.6 `util/workqueue` \u2014 Work Queue\n\n**Directory**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/`\n\nA purpose-built queue that deduplicates work items and enables controlled retry with backoff. Described in Section 3.\n\n### 2.7 `tools/leaderelection` \u2014 High-Availability Coordination\n\n**File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/leaderelection.go`\n\nA library for leader election based on `coordination.k8s.io/Lease` objects. Used by the controller manager and scheduler to ensure single-writer semantics across replicated deployments.\n\n### 2.8 `tools/clientcmd` \u2014 Kubeconfig Parsing\n\n**Directory**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/clientcmd/`\n\nHandles all kubeconfig file parsing, context merging, and external credential plugin invocation. The canonical pattern is `clientcmd.BuildConfigFromFlags(\"\", kubeconfig)` \u2192 `rest.Config`.\n\n---\n\n## 3. End-to-End Control Flow\n\n### 3.1 How a Request Reaches the API Server\n\nA typed client call like `clientset.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").Get(ctx, \"my-pod\", metav1.GetOptions{})` proceeds through these layers:\n\n1. **Typed client** translates the call into a `rest.Request` with verb=`GET`, resource=`pods`, namespace=`default`, name=`my-pod`.\n2. **`rest.Request.Do(ctx)`** acquires a token from the `flowcontrol.RateLimiter` (blocking if the QPS limit is exceeded), then calls `r.c.Client.Do(req)`.\n3. **Transport chain** (assembled by `transport.New`): the request passes through each `http.RoundTripper` wrapper in order. These add the `Authorization` header, perform TLS, and may retry on `429 Too Many Requests` by reading the `Retry-After` header.\n4. **Response handling**: the client deserializes the response body using the `NegotiatedSerializer`. On non-2xx, it constructs a `errors.StatusError` from the server's `metav1.Status` response body, enabling callers to use `apierrors.IsNotFound(err)`, `IsConflict(err)`, etc.\n\nContent negotiation happens via the HTTP `Accept` header. By default JSON is used; Protobuf can be requested for lower wire overhead. Metadata-only requests use `Accept: application/json;as=PartialObjectMetadata`.\n\n### 3.2 The Informer Pipeline: Event-Driven Cache Maintenance\n\nThe controller infrastructure in `tools/cache` replaces polling with a LIST+WATCH mechanism. The data flows through four sequential stages:\n\n```\nAPI Server\n    |  LIST (paginated, ResourceVersion=0)\n    |  WATCH (from returned ResourceVersion)\n    v\nReflector  (tools/cache/reflector.go)\n    |  Puts Added/Updated/Deleted/Replaced/Sync deltas\n    v\nDeltaFIFO  (tools/cache/delta_fifo.go)\n    |  Popped by the internal controller loop\n    |  HandleDeltas() called with DeltaFIFO's lock held\n    v\nIndexer (ThreadSafeStore)  (tools/cache/thread_safe_store.go)\n    |  Cache updated atomically; index rebuilt\n    |  Notification sent to sharedProcessor\n    v\nsharedProcessor \u2192 processListener(s)  (tools/cache/shared_informer.go)\n    |  OnAdd / OnUpdate / OnDelete called on each registered handler\n    v\nController business logic\n    |  Typically: enqueue object key into workqueue\n    v\nWork Queue  (util/workqueue/)\n    |  Deduplicated, rate-limited\n    v\nReconcile loop\n    |  Reads from Lister (cache, not API server)\n    v\nAPI Server mutation (if needed)\n```\n\n#### Stage 1: Reflector (`tools/cache/reflector.go`)\n\nThe `Reflector` owns the connection to the API server. When started, it calls `ListAndWatchWithContext` (`reflector.go:408`):\n\n1. **List phase**: Calls `r.list(ctx)`, which invokes the `ListerWatcher.ListWithContext` in a goroutine. Results arrive in pages (the pager utility in `tools/pager` handles continuation tokens). When the page loop ends, the Reflector captures the `resourceVersion` from the list response and calls `r.store.Replace()` on the `DeltaFIFO`.\n\n2. **Watch phase**: Starts a watch request from the captured `resourceVersion` (`reflector.go:524-548`). The `AllowWatchBookmarks: true` flag (`reflector.go:533`) enables the server to send `BOOKMARK` events, which carry a fresh `resourceVersion` without representing an object change. This allows the Reflector to advance its `lastSyncResourceVersion` even during quiet periods, enabling faster recovery from watch expiry.\n\n3. **Recovery**: If the watch returns an \"expired\" error (410 Gone), the Reflector re-lists. The `backoffManager` at `reflector.go:282` implements exponential backoff from 800ms to 30s, resetting after 2 minutes of stable operation.\n\n4. **WatchList mode**: When the `WatchListClient` feature gate is enabled, `ListAndWatchWithContext` calls `r.watchList(ctx)` instead of `r.list(ctx)`, streaming the initial state via a watch rather than a paginated list. This reduces memory pressure on the API server (`reflector.go:421-436`).\n\nThe `Reflector` struct definition (`reflector.go:87-148`) stores the `ListerWatcherWithContext` interface. This interface has two methods, `ListWithContext` and `WatchWithContext`, and is the primary extension point for testing and custom data sources (`listwatch.go:42-87`).\n\n#### Stage 2: DeltaFIFO (`tools/cache/delta_fifo.go`)\n\n`DeltaFIFO` is a producer-consumer queue where each entry is a `Deltas`\u2014a slice of `Delta{Type, Object}` for a single object key (`delta_fifo.go:104-146`). Delta types are `Added`, `Updated`, `Deleted`, `Replaced`, and `Sync` (`delta_fifo.go:170-183`).\n\nThe critical design property: **deltas for the same key are coalesced**. If an object is updated twice before the consumer processes it, the queue holds both updates under a single key entry. When the consumer pops that key, it receives both deltas in order. This prevents unbounded queue growth during bursts while preserving the semantic ordering of changes.\n\n`knownObjects` (`delta_fifo.go:130`) is a reference to the downstream Indexer. When `Replace()` is called (on list completion), the DeltaFIFO computes the set difference between the incoming list and `knownObjects` to synthesise `Deleted` events for objects that were removed while the watch was down.\n\n#### Stage 3: Indexer and ThreadSafeStore (`tools/cache/thread_safe_store.go`)\n\nThe `Indexer` interface (`index.go:35-55`) extends `Store` with multi-dimensional lookup:\n\n```go\ntype Indexer interface {\n    Store\n    Index(indexName string, obj interface{}) ([]interface{}, error)\n    IndexKeys(indexName, indexedValue string) ([]string, error)\n    ByIndex(indexName, indexedValue string) ([]interface{}, error)\n    AddIndexers(newIndexers Indexers) error\n}\n```\n\nThe backing implementation is `threadSafeMap` in `thread_safe_store.go`, which holds a `map[string]interface{}` for objects and a `storeIndex` for secondary indices. A `sync.RWMutex` guards both. The default index is `NamespaceIndex` keyed by `MetaNamespaceIndexFunc` (`index.go:84-91`).\n\n`TransformFunc` (`delta_fifo.go:148-164`) is an optional hook registered on `DeltaFIFO` that runs on each object before it is inserted into the Indexer. Its primary use is memory reduction: stripping metadata fields (managed fields, annotations) that the controller does not need.\n\n#### Stage 4: sharedProcessor and Event Delivery (`tools/cache/shared_informer.go`)\n\n`sharedIndexInformer.HandleDeltas` (the `Process` function wired into the internal `controller`) is called with the DeltaFIFO lock held. For each delta it:\n1. Updates the Indexer (cache).\n2. Calls `s.processor.distribute(notification, false)`.\n\nThe `sharedProcessor` holds a list of `processListener`s, one per registered `ResourceEventHandler`. Each listener has an internal ring buffer and a dedicated goroutine. When `distribute` is called, it writes the notification into each listener's buffer. The goroutine drains the buffer and calls the handler's `OnAdd`, `OnUpdate`, or `OnDelete`.\n\n**Notification ordering guarantee**: Within a single listener, notifications are delivered in order. Across listeners, delivery is concurrent. This is why handler implementations must be non-blocking\u2014slow handlers back up their own buffer but do not block the informer's internal loop or other listeners.\n\nA handler added after the informer has started receives a synthetic batch of `OnAdd` events for every object currently in the cache (`shared_informer.go:696-700`):\n```go\n// shared_informer.go:698\ns.blockDeltas.Lock()\ndefer s.blockDeltas.Unlock()\n```\nThe `blockDeltas` mutex pauses all ongoing notifications while the late-joining handler is caught up.\n\n### 3.3 SharedInformerFactory: Lifecycle Management\n\n`informers.NewSharedInformerFactory` (`informers/factory.go:112`) returns a `sharedInformerFactory` that deduplicates informers by type. All calls to `factory.Core().V1().Pods().Informer()` return the same underlying `SharedIndexInformer` regardless of how many callers request it. When `factory.Start(stopCh)` is called, it starts a goroutine per unique informer type (`factory.go:144`).\n\n### 3.4 Work Queue and Reconciliation Loop\n\nThe canonical controller pattern: event handlers only enqueue the object's key, never process it inline.\n\n```\ninformer.AddEventHandler(cache.ResourceEventHandlerFuncs{\n    AddFunc:    func(obj interface{}) { queue.Add(keyOf(obj)) },\n    UpdateFunc: func(old, new interface{}) { queue.Add(keyOf(new)) },\n    DeleteFunc: func(obj interface{}) { queue.Add(keyOf(obj)) },\n})\n```\n\nThe `Typed[T]` queue in `util/workqueue/queue.go` maintains three concurrent sets:\n- `queue`: ordered slice of items waiting to be processed\n- `dirty`: set of all items that need processing (superset of queue)\n- `processing`: set of items currently held by a worker\n\nWhen `Add(item)` is called (`queue.go:227`): if `item` is already in `dirty`, it is a no-op for the queue ordering but the item is marked dirty for re-processing after the current work completes. When `Get()` returns an item, it moves from `dirty` to `processing`. `Done(item)` removes it from `processing`; if the item is still in `dirty` (was re-added while being processed), it is re-queued.\n\n**Rate-limiting layer**: `TypedRateLimitingInterface` (`rate_limiting_queue.go:27`) wraps the base queue with `AddRateLimited(item)`, which calls `queue.AddAfter(item, rateLimiter.When(item))`.\n\nThe default rate limiter (`default_rate_limiters.go:50-56`) is a `MaxOfRateLimiter` combining:\n1. `ItemExponentialFailureRateLimiter`: per-item backoff starting at 5ms, doubling on each failure, capping at 1000s.\n2. `BucketRateLimiter`: an overall token bucket at 10 QPS, burst 100.\n\nThe controller calls `queue.Forget(item)` on success to reset the per-item failure count.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 4.1 Watch Expiry and the Relist Cycle\n\nThe API server evicts watch events from its etcd watch cache after a bounded window (typically 5 minutes). A Reflector that is partitioned or slow to consume will receive a `410 Gone` error, after which it must re-list from scratch. A relist is expensive: it fetches every object of that type from etcd, bypassing the watch cache. In large clusters this can cause etcd load spikes and memory pressure on the API server.\n\n**Mitigation**: The Reflector uses watch bookmarks (`AllowWatchBookmarks: true`) to advance `lastSyncResourceVersion` continuously. On re-list, it uses the most recent known `resourceVersion` rather than `0`, allowing the API server to serve the list from its watch cache rather than etcd. The WatchList feature gate (`clientfeatures.WatchListClient`) eliminates the list phase entirely by streaming the initial state via a watch.\n\n### 4.2 Missed Deletions During Relist\n\nBetween a watch expiry and the next successful list response, objects can be deleted. `DeltaFIFO.Replace()` handles this by computing the difference between the new list and `knownObjects`. Objects present in `knownObjects` but absent from the incoming list receive a synthesised `Deleted` delta. If `EmitDeltaTypeReplaced` is false (the default for historical compatibility), these are emitted as `Sync` rather than `Replaced`, making it impossible for downstream handlers to distinguish a relist from a periodic resync.\n\n### 4.3 Slow Event Handlers Block Their Own Queue\n\nA `processListener`'s buffer has a fixed initial size (`initialBufferSize = 1024`, `shared_informer.go:340`). If a handler is slow, the buffer grows unboundedly because `distribute` uses the buffer's `WriteOne` which grows it. The handler goroutine will eventually apply backpressure to the `HandleDeltas` path. If all listener buffers are full simultaneously, the entire informer stalls\u2014no new events from the API server will be processed. The contract \"handlers must process promptly\" is documented at `shared_informer.go:130-134`.\n\n### 4.4 Client-Side Rate Limiting and the 429 Trap\n\n`rest.Config` defaults to `QPS=5.0, Burst=10`. A controller making many rapid updates (e.g., patching status on every reconcile) will be throttled client-side. When the token bucket is exhausted, calls block. Callers that share a single `Clientset` share a single rate limiter. In high-throughput controllers, it is standard practice to set `QPS` and `Burst` explicitly (e.g., 100/200).\n\nThe REST client also handles server-side throttling: HTTP 429 responses trigger a read of the `Retry-After` header, followed by a sleep and retry (`rest/with_retry.go`).\n\n### 4.5 Leader Election Does Not Guarantee Exclusivity\n\n`tools/leaderelection` documents explicitly (`leaderelection.go:20`): \"This implementation does not guarantee that only one client is acting as a leader.\" The election uses a `Lease` resource; a slow leader might fail to renew within `RenewDeadline` and lose the lease while still running. The typical guard is to check `IsLeader()` before every mutation, but this is still a window. Callers that need strict fencing must implement additional checks (e.g., resource-version validation on writes).\n\nThe timing parameters have a strict relationship enforced at construction (`leaderelection.go:77-91`):\n- `LeaseDuration > RenewDeadline > RetryPeriod * JitterFactor (1.2)`\n- Kubernetes defaults: LeaseDuration=15s, RenewDeadline=10s, RetryPeriod=2s\n\n### 4.6 Indexer Mutation Safety\n\n`ThreadSafeStore`'s contract (`thread_safe_store.go:34-42`) is that callers must treat returned objects as **read-only**. A `Get` returns the stored pointer directly, not a copy. Mutating it corrupts both the cache and any secondary indices built on that object's fields. The `MutationDetector` (`tools/cache/mutation_detector.go`) can be enabled in development to catch violations, but it has significant overhead and is disabled by default.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Extension Points, Customization Hooks, and Associated Risks\n\n### 5.1 `WrapTransport` / Custom `RoundTripper`\n\nSetting `rest.Config.WrapTransport` inserts an `http.RoundTripper` into every request. This is the correct place for cross-cutting concerns like logging, tracing, or mutual auth. The wrapper is called after authentication is set up, so it sees auth headers. Risk: a misbehaving wrapper that does not call the next transport will silently drop all requests.\n\n### 5.2 `TransformFunc` on DeltaFIFO / SharedIndexInformer\n\n```go\n// Registered via SharedInformer.SetTransform() or SharedInformerFactory.WithTransform()\nfactory := informers.NewSharedInformerFactoryWithOptions(\n    client, resyncPeriod,\n    informers.WithTransform(stripUnneededFields),\n)\n```\n\nThe transform runs inside the DeltaFIFO pop path, before the object is stored in the Indexer. It must be fast (it runs under the DeltaFIFO lock) and must be idempotent. A transform that modifies the object in place and is then re-applied during a resync will corrupt the object if it is not idempotent. As of v1.27, mutating in place is explicitly safe (`delta_fifo.go:153-155`).\n\n### 5.3 Custom Indexers\n\n```go\ninformer.AddIndexers(cache.Indexers{\n    \"byServiceAccount\": func(obj interface{}) ([]string, error) { ... },\n})\n```\n\nIndexers enable O(1) lookup by arbitrary fields (`index.go:53-54`). They must be added before the informer starts (`shared_informer.go:616-625`). Risk: an `IndexFunc` that panics or returns an error leaves the Indexer in an inconsistent state for that object's secondary index entry.\n\n### 5.4 `WatchErrorHandler` / `WatchErrorHandlerWithContext`\n\n```go\ninformer.SetWatchErrorHandlerWithContext(func(ctx context.Context, r *cache.Reflector, err error) {\n    // visibility hook; called in a non-blocking context\n})\n```\n\nThis hook is called when the Reflector drops its watch connection. It is for observability only and must return quickly (`shared_informer.go:207-213`). Setting it after the informer has started returns an error (`shared_informer.go:501-511`).\n\n### 5.5 `ListerWatcher` \u2014 Custom Data Sources\n\nThe `ListerWatcher` interface is what the Reflector uses to talk to the API server. Any type implementing `ListWithContext` and `WatchWithContext` can be substituted:\n\n```go\ntype ListerWatcherWithContext interface {\n    ListerWithContext\n    WatcherWithContext\n}\n```\n\nThis is the foundation for the `testing/` fake infrastructure and for synthetic informers (e.g., the metadata-only informer in `metadata/metadatainformer/`).\n\n### 5.6 Custom Rate Limiters on Work Queue\n\nThe default `NewTypedRateLimitingQueue` accepts any `TypedRateLimiter[T]`. Controllers with different retry profiles can substitute `ItemBucketRateLimiter`, a fixed-delay limiter, or a custom implementation. The key requirement is that `Forget(item)` resets the per-item state; failing to call it means the item's backoff grows forever, eventually causing it to take 1000s to retry.\n\n### 5.7 Server-Side Apply via `applyconfigurations`\n\n`applyconfigurations/` contains generated, type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply patches. Instead of the get-modify-update pattern (which requires a resource-version match and conflicts under concurrent actors), a controller can declare ownership of specific fields:\n\n```go\npatch := applycorev1.Pod(\"my-pod\", \"default\").\n    WithSpec(applycorev1.PodSpec().WithContainers(...))\nclient.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").Apply(ctx, patch, metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: \"my-controller\"})\n```\n\nThe server merges the patch against existing fields owned by other field managers. This eliminates the optimistic concurrency retry loop for controllers that fully own their fields. Risk: misunderstanding field ownership can cause unexpected field deletion\u2014the server treats the absence of a field in the patch as an intent to remove it.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Operational Implications\n\n### 6.1 Memory Footprint of the Informer Cache\n\nEach `SharedIndexInformer` stores the full deserialized object for every resource of the watched type. A cluster with 100,000 Pods where each Pod object is 20KB of JSON will consume roughly 2GB in the informer cache, per process. This is the primary memory driver for the controller manager and scheduler.\n\n**Mitigation options**:\n- Use `TransformFunc` to zero out fields the controller does not need (e.g., `ManagedFields`, `Annotations`).\n- Use the `metadata.Interface` client (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/metadata/`) to watch only `ObjectMeta`, discarding spec and status entirely. The backing informer sends `Accept: application/json;as=PartialObjectMetadata`.\n\n### 6.2 API Server Load from the Reflector\n\nEach Reflector opens one persistent watch connection to the API server per resource type per process. The API server maintains state for each watch connection. For a large deployment with many controller replicas watching many resource types, the number of concurrent watchers can stress the API server's etcd watcher infrastructure.\n\nThe `SharedInformerFactory` pattern is the mitigation: all handlers for a given resource type share a single underlying Reflector, reducing watcher count from O(handlers) to O(resource types) per process.\n\n### 6.3 Reconciliation Queue Depth as a Reliability Signal\n\nThe `util/workqueue` exposes Prometheus metrics (depth, adds, latency, work duration, retries, unfinished work) via the `MetricsProvider` interface (`workqueue/metrics.go`). Queue depth spiking while work duration stays flat indicates an event flood from the informer (e.g., a rapid-fire field churn). Work duration increasing while depth stays flat indicates the reconcile loop itself has slowed (likely due to API server latency).\n\n### 6.4 Leader Election Tuning\n\nAggressive `LeaseDuration` / `RenewDeadline` ratios increase the frequency of API server writes (Lease renewals every `RetryPeriod` while leading) and increase failover time (new leader must wait `LeaseDuration` before taking over). In high-latency environments (cross-zone or cross-region API servers), the defaults (15s/10s/2s) may cause spurious leader transitions if renewal calls exceed `RenewDeadline`. Increasing these values trades latency for stability.\n\n### 6.5 Fake Clients in Tests\n\n`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/testing/` provides a reaction-based fake framework (`Fake`, `ObjectTracker`, `ReactionChain`). The typed fake client (`kubernetes/fake/`) is generated alongside the real client. It satisfies the `kubernetes.Interface` but stores objects in memory. Because it does not enforce `resourceVersion` incrementing or server-side validation, tests that rely on it can miss bugs that appear only with a real API server.\n\n---\n\n## 7. File Map\n\nThe following paths are the most important entry points for understanding and modifying client-go. All paths are relative to the repository root.\n\n| Concern | Path |\n|---|---|\n| Client configuration and construction | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/config.go` |\n| HTTP request builder | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/request.go` |\n| Transport layer (TLS, auth middleware) | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/transport/transport.go` |\n| Typed clientset (generated) | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/kubernetes/` |\n| Dynamic (unstructured) client interface | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/dynamic/interface.go` |\n| Reflector: LIST/WATCH loop | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/reflector.go` |\n| DeltaFIFO: event accumulation queue | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/delta_fifo.go` |\n| SharedIndexInformer: event distribution | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/shared_informer.go` |\n| Internal controller (Reflector wiring) | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/controller.go` |\n| Indexer interface and index types | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/index.go` |\n| ThreadSafeStore (cache backend) | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/thread_safe_store.go` |\n| ListerWatcher interface | | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go` |\n| Leader election | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/leaderelection.go` |\n| Server-side apply configurations | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/` |\n| Metadata-only client | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/metadata/` |\n| Kubeconfig parsing | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/clientcmd/` |\n| Fake client infrastructure | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/testing/` |\n| Architecture overview (upstream doc) | `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md` |\n",
  "file_path": "/workspace/documentation.md"
